<p><b>Advanced Micro Devices, Inc.</b> (<b>AMD</b>) is an American <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Multinational_company" title="Multinational company">multinational</a> <a href="/wiki/Semiconductor_industry" title="Semiconductor industry">semiconductor company</a> based in <a href="/wiki/Sunnyvale,_California" title="Sunnyvale, California">Sunnyvale</a>, <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a>, United States, that develops computer processors and related technologies for business and consumer markets. While initially it manufactured its own processors, the company became <a href="/wiki/Fabless_manufacturing" title="Fabless manufacturing">fabless</a> after <a href="/wiki/GlobalFoundries" title="GlobalFoundries">GlobalFoundries</a> was spun off in 2009. AMD's main products include <a href="/wiki/Microprocessor" title="Microprocessor">microprocessors</a>, <a href="/wiki/Motherboard" title="Motherboard">motherboard</a> <a href="/wiki/Chipset" title="Chipset">chipsets</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Embedded_processor" title="Embedded processor">embedded processors</a> and <a href="/wiki/Graphics_processing_unit" title="Graphics processing unit">graphics processors</a> for <a href="/wiki/Server_(computing)" title="Server (computing)">servers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Workstation" title="Workstation">workstations</a> and personal computers, and <a href="/wiki/Embedded_system" title="Embedded system">embedded systems</a> applications.</p>
<p>AMD is the second-largest supplier and only significant rival to <a href="/wiki/Intel" title="Intel">Intel</a> in the market for <a href="/wiki/X86" title="X86">x86</a>-based microprocessors. Since acquiring <a href="/wiki/ATI_Technologies" title="ATI Technologies">ATI</a> in 2006, AMD and its competitor <a href="/wiki/Nvidia" title="Nvidia">Nvidia</a> have dominated the discrete Graphics Processor Unit (GPU) market.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Company_history">Company history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Advanced_Micro_Devices&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Company history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:AMDmarkham4.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2112" data-file-width="2816" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/AMDmarkham4.jpg/220px-AMDmarkham4.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/AMDmarkham4.jpg/330px-AMDmarkham4.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/AMDmarkham4.jpg/440px-AMDmarkham4.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>

</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">

</div>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="First_twelve_years">First twelve years</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Advanced_Micro_Devices&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: First twelve years">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Advanced Micro Devices was formally incorporated on May 1, 1969, by <a href="/wiki/Jerry_Sanders_(businessman)" title="Jerry Sanders (businessman)">Jerry Sanders</a>, along with seven of his colleagues from <a href="/wiki/Fairchild_Semiconductor" title="Fairchild Semiconductor">Fairchild Semiconductor</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> Sanders, an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_engineer" title="Electrical engineer">electrical engineer</a> who was the director of marketing at Fairchild, had like many Fairchild executives grown frustrated with the increasing lack of support, opportunity, and flexibility within that company, and decided to leave to start his own <a href="/wiki/Semiconductor" title="Semiconductor">semiconductor</a> company.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> The previous year <a href="/wiki/Robert_Noyce" title="Robert Noyce">Robert Noyce</a>, who had invented the first practical <a href="/wiki/Integrated_circuit" title="Integrated circuit">integrated circuit</a> or microchip in 1959 at Fairchild,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> had left Fairchild together with <a href="/wiki/Gordon_Moore" title="Gordon Moore">Gordon Moore</a> and founded the semiconductor company <a href="/wiki/Intel" title="Intel">Intel</a> in July 1968.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abc_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-abc-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>In September 1969, AMD moved from its temporary location in <a href="/wiki/Santa_Clara,_California" title="Santa Clara, California">Santa Clara</a> to <a href="/wiki/Sunnyvale,_California" title="Sunnyvale, California">Sunnyvale</a>, California.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> To immediately secure a customer base, AMD initially became a <a href="/wiki/Second_source" title="Second source">second source</a> supplier of microchips designed by Fairchild and <a href="/wiki/National_Semiconductor" title="National Semiconductor">National Semiconductor</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-directory_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-directory-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> AMD first focused on producing logic chips.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> The company guaranteed quality control to <a href="/wiki/MIL-STD-883" title="MIL-STD-883">United States Military Standard</a>, an advantage in the early computer industry since unreliability in microchips was a distinct problem that customers – including computer manufacturers, the telecommunications industry, and instrument manufacturers – wanted to avoid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-directory_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-directory-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-singer_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-singer-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-svh_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-svh-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<p>In November 1969, the company manufactured its first product, the Am9300, a <a href="/wiki/4-bit" title="4-bit">4-bit</a> <a href="/wiki/Integrated_circuit#SSI.2C_MSI_and_LSI" title="Integrated circuit">MSI</a> <a href="/wiki/Shift_register" title="Shift register">shift register</a>, which began selling in 1970.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-svh_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-svh-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> Also in 1970, AMD produced its first proprietary product, the Am2501 logic counter, which was highly successful.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lojek220_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-lojek220-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-history_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-history-21">[21]</a></sup> Its best-selling product in 1971 was the Am2505, the fastest <a href="/wiki/Binary_multiplier" title="Binary multiplier">multiplier</a> available.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lojek220_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-lojek220-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rodengen41_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-rodengen41-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1971, AMD entered the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/RAM" title="RAM">RAM</a> chip market, beginning with the Am3101, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/64-bit" title="64-bit">64-bit</a> bipolar RAM.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rodengen41_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-rodengen41-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> That year AMD also greatly increased the sales volume of its linear integrated circuits, and by year end the company's total annual sales reached $4.6 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lojek220_20-2"><a href="#cite_note-lojek220-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p>AMD went public in September 1972.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-directory_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-directory-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> The company was a second source for Intel <a href="/wiki/MOSFET" title="MOSFET">MOS</a>/<a href="/wiki/Integrated_circuit#LSI" title="Integrated circuit">LSI</a> circuits by 1973, with products such as Am14/1506 and Am14/1507, dual 100-bit dynamic shift registers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> By 1975, AMD was producing 212 products – of which 49 were proprietary, including the Am9102 (a <a href="/wiki/Static_random-access_memory" title="Static random-access memory">static</a> <a href="/wiki/Extrinsic_semiconductor#N-type_semiconductors" title="Extrinsic semiconductor">N-channel</a> 1024-bit RAM)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> and three low-power <a href="/wiki/Schottky_barrier" title="Schottky barrier">Schottky</a> <a href="/wiki/Integrated_circuit#Generations" title="Integrated circuit">MSI</a> circuits: Am25LS07, Am25LS08, and Am25LS09.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rodengen55_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-rodengen55-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<p>Intel had created the first <a href="/wiki/Microprocessor" title="Microprocessor">microprocessor</a>, its 4-bit <a href="/wiki/Intel_4004" title="Intel 4004">4004</a>, in 1971.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> By 1975, AMD entered the microprocessor market with the <a href="/wiki/AMD_Am9080" title="AMD Am9080">Am9080</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Reverse_engineering" title="Reverse engineering">reverse-engineered</a> clone of the <a href="/wiki/Intel_8080" title="Intel 8080">Intel 8080</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rodengen50_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-rodengen50-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/AMD_Am2900" title="AMD Am2900">Am2900</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bit-slice" title="Bit-slice">bit-slice</a> microprocessor family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rodengen50_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-rodengen50-34">[34]</a></sup> When Intel began installing <a href="/wiki/Microcode" title="Microcode">microcode</a> in its microprocessors in 1976, it entered into a <a href="/wiki/Cross-licensing" title="Cross-licensing">cross-licensing</a> agreement with AMD, granting AMD a copyright license to the microcode in its microprocessors and peripherals, effective October 1976.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rodengen55_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-rodengen55-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-justia1994_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-justia1994-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sec1996_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-sec1996-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1977, AMD entered into a joint venture with <a href="/wiki/Siemens" title="Siemens">Siemens</a>, a German engineering conglomerate wishing to enhance its technology expertise and enter the U.S. market.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-malerba_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-malerba-40">[40]</a></sup> Siemens purchased 20% of AMD's stock, giving AMD an infusion of cash to increase its product lines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-malerba_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-malerba-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> That year the two companies also jointly established Advanced Micro Computers, located in Silicon Valley and in Germany, giving AMD an opportunity to enter the <a href="/wiki/Microcomputer" title="Microcomputer">microcomputer</a> development and manufacturing field,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-malerba_40-2"><a href="#cite_note-malerba-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rodengen60_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-rodengen60-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> in particular based on AMD's second-source <a href="/wiki/Zilog" title="Zilog">Zilog</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Z8000" title="Z8000">Z8000</a> microprocessors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-freiberger_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-freiberger-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> When the two companies' vision for Advanced Micro Computers diverged, AMD bought out Siemens' stake in the U.S. division in 1979.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> AMD closed its Advanced Micro Computers subsidiary in late 1981, after switching focus to manufacturing second-source Intel x86 microprocessors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-freiberger_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-freiberger-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-swaine_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-swaine-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p>Total sales in <a href="/wiki/Fiscal_year" title="Fiscal year">fiscal year</a> 1978 topped $100 million,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rodengen60_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-rodengen60-43">[43]</a></sup> and in 1979, AMD debuted on the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Stock_Exchange" title="New York Stock Exchange">New York Stock Exchange</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-history_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-history-21">[21]</a></sup> In 1979, production also began in AMD's new semiconductor <a href="/wiki/Semiconductor_fabrication_plant" title="Semiconductor fabrication plant">fab</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Austin" title="Austin">Austin</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-history_21-2"><a href="#cite_note-history-21">[21]</a></sup> the company already had overseas assembly facilities in <a href="/wiki/Penang" title="Penang">Penang</a> and <a href="/wiki/Manila" title="Manila">Manila</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> and it began construction on a semiconductor fab in <a href="/wiki/San_Antonio" title="San Antonio">San Antonio</a> in 1981.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HittC26_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-HittC26-53">[53]</a></sup> In 1980, AMD began supplying semiconductor products for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Telecommunications" title="Telecommunications">telecommunications</a>, an industry undergoing rapid expansion and innovation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Technology_exchange_agreement_with_Intel">Technology exchange agreement with Intel</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Advanced_Micro_Devices&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Technology exchange agreement with Intel">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Intel had introduced the first <a href="/wiki/X86" title="X86">x86</a> microprocessors in 1978.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> In 1981, <a href="/wiki/IBM" title="IBM">IBM</a> created its <a href="/wiki/IBM_Personal_Computer" title="IBM Personal Computer">PC</a>, and wanted Intel's x86 processors, but only under the condition that Intel also provide a second-source manufacturer for its patented x86 microprocessors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-singer_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-singer-16">[16]</a></sup> Intel and AMD entered into a 10-year technology exchange agreement, first signed in October 1981<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-swaine_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-swaine-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> and formally executed in February 1982.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sec1996_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-sec1996-39">[39]</a></sup> The terms of the agreement were that each company could acquire the right to become a second-source manufacturer for semiconductor products developed by the other; that is, each party could "earn" the right to manufacture and sell a product developed by the other, if agreed to, by exchanging the manufacturing rights to a product of equivalent technical complexity. The technical information and licenses needed to make and sell a part would be exchanged for a royalty to the developing company.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-justia1994_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-justia1994-38">[38]</a></sup> The 1982 agreement also extended the 1976 AMD–Intel cross-licensing agreement through 1995.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-justia1994_38-2"><a href="#cite_note-justia1994-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sec1996_39-2"><a href="#cite_note-sec1996-39">[39]</a></sup> The agreement included the right to invoke arbitration of disagreements, and after five years the right of either party to end the agreement with one year's notice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-justia1994_38-3"><a href="#cite_note-justia1994-38">[38]</a></sup> The main result of the 1982 agreement was that AMD became a second-source manufacturer of Intel's x86 microprocessors and related chips, and Intel provided AMD with database tapes for its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/8086" title="8086">8086</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/80186" title="80186">80186</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/80286" title="80286">80286</a> chips.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sec1996_39-3"><a href="#cite_note-sec1996-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<p>Beginning in 1982, AMD began volume-producing second-source Intel-licensed 8086, 8088, 80186, and 80188 processors, and by 1984 its own <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Am286" title="Am286">Am286</a> clone of Intel's 80286 processor, for the rapidly growing market of IBM PCs and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IBM_clone" title="IBM clone">IBM clones</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-singer_16-2"><a href="#cite_note-singer-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> It also continued its successful concentration on proprietary <a href="/wiki/Bipolar_junction_transistor" title="Bipolar junction transistor">bipolar</a> chips.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> In 1983, it introduced INT.STD.1000, the highest manufacturing quality standard in the industry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-svh_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-svh-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HittC26_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-HittC26-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<p>The company continued to spend greatly on research and development,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> and in addition to other breakthrough products, created the world's first 512K <a href="/wiki/EPROM" title="EPROM">EPROM</a> in 1984.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> That year AMD was listed in the book <i>The 100 Best Companies to Work for in America</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HittC26_53-2"><a href="#cite_note-HittC26-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> and based on 1984 income it made the <a href="/wiki/Fortune_500" title="Fortune 500"><i>Fortune</i> 500</a> list for the first time in 1985.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup></p>
<p>By mid-1985, however, the microchip market experienced a severe downturn, mainly due to longterm aggressive trade practices (<a href="/wiki/Dumping_(pricing_policy)" title="Dumping (pricing policy)">dumping</a>) from Japan, but also due to a crowded and non-innovative chip market in the U.S.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> AMD rode out the mid-1980s crisis by aggressively innovating and modernizing,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> devising the Liberty Chip program of designing and manufacturing one new chip or chip set per week for 52 weeks in <a href="/wiki/Fiscal_year" title="Fiscal year">fiscal year</a> 1986,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HittC26_53-3"><a href="#cite_note-HittC26-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> and by heavily lobbying the U.S. government until sanctions and restrictions were put into place to prevent predatory Japanese pricing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> During this time period, AMD withdrew from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/DRAM" title="DRAM">DRAM</a> market,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> and at the same time made some headway into the <a href="/wiki/CMOS" title="CMOS">CMOS</a> market, which it had lagged in entering, having focused instead on bipolar chips.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<p>AMD had some success in the mid-1980s with the AMD7910 and AMD7911 "World Chip" <a href="/wiki/Frequency-shift_keying" title="Frequency-shift keying">FSK</a> modem, one of the first multi-standard devices that covered both Bell and <a href="/wiki/ITU-T" title="ITU-T">CCITT</a> tones at up to 1200 baud half duplex or 300/300 full duplex.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> Beginning in 1986, AMD embraced the perceived shift toward <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/RISC" title="RISC">RISC</a> with their own <a href="/wiki/AMD_Am29000" title="AMD Am29000">AMD Am29000</a> (29k) processor;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> the 29k survived as an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Embedded_processor" title="Embedded processor">embedded processor</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> The company also increased its <a href="/wiki/EPROM" title="EPROM">EPROM</a> memory market share in the late 1980s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> Throughout the 1980s, AMD was a second-source supplier of Intel x86 processors. In 1991, it introduced its own 386-compatible <a href="/wiki/Am386" title="Am386">Am386</a>, an AMD-designed chip. Creating its own chips, AMD began to compete directly with Intel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<p>AMD had a large and successful <a href="/wiki/Flash_memory" title="Flash memory">flash memory</a> business, even during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dotcom_bust" title="Dotcom bust">dotcom bust</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> In 2003, to divest some manufacturing and aid its overall cash flow, which was under duress from aggressive microprocessor competition from Intel, AMD spun-off its flash memory business and manufacturing into <a href="/wiki/Spansion" title="Spansion">Spansion</a>, a joint venture with <a href="/wiki/Fujitsu" title="Fujitsu">Fujitsu</a>, which had been co-manufacturing flash memory with AMD since 1993.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> AMD divested itself of Spansion in December 2005, in order to focus on the microprocessor market, and Spansion went public in an IPO.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<p>AMD announced the acquisition of the graphics processor company <a href="/wiki/ATI_Technologies" title="ATI Technologies">ATI Technologies</a> on July 24, 2006. AMD paid $4.3 billion in cash and 58 million shares of its <a href="/wiki/Stock" title="Stock">stock</a>, for a total of approximately $5.4 billion. The transaction completed on October 25, 2006.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> On August 30, 2010, AMD announced that it would retire the ATI brand name for its graphics chipsets in favor of the AMD brand name.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup></p>
<p>In October 2008, AMD announced plans to spin off manufacturing operations in the form of a multibillion-dollar joint venture with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Advanced_Technology_Investment_Company" title="Advanced Technology Investment Company">Advanced Technology Investment Co.</a>, an investment company formed by the government of <a href="/wiki/Abu_Dhabi" title="Abu Dhabi">Abu Dhabi</a>. The new venture is called <a href="/wiki/GlobalFoundries" title="GlobalFoundries">GlobalFoundries Inc.</a> The partnership and spin-off gave AMD an infusion of cash and allowed AMD to focus solely on chip design.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> To assure the Abu Dhabi investors of the new venture's success, CEO <a href="/wiki/Hector_Ruiz" title="Hector Ruiz">Hector Ruiz</a> stepped down as CEO of AMD in July 2008, while remaining Executive Chairman, in preparation to becoming Chairman of Global Foundries in March 2009.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> President and COO <a href="/wiki/Dirk_Meyer" title="Dirk Meyer">Dirk Meyer</a> became AMD's CEO.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> Recessionary losses necessitated AMD cutting 1,100 jobs in 2009.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<p>In August 2011, AMD announced that former <a href="/wiki/Lenovo" title="Lenovo">Lenovo</a> executive <a href="/wiki/Rory_Read" title="Rory Read">Rory Read</a> would be joining the company as CEO, replacing Meyer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> AMD announced in November 2011 plans to lay off more than 10% (1,400) of its employees from across all divisions worldwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> In October 2012, it announced plans to lay off an additional 15% of its workforce to reduce costs in the face of declining sales revenue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-king2012_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-king2012-90">[90]</a></sup></p>
<p>AMD acquired the low-power server manufacturer <a href="/wiki/SeaMicro" title="SeaMicro">SeaMicro</a> in early 2012, with an eye to bringing out an <a href="/wiki/ARM_architecture" title="ARM architecture">ARM architecture</a> server chip.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vance2012_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-vance2012-91">[91]</a></sup></p>
<p>On October 8, 2014, AMD announced that Rory Read had stepped down after three years as president and chief executive officer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wsj.com_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-wsj.com-2">[2]</a></sup> He was succeeded by <a href="/wiki/Lisa_Su" title="Lisa Su">Lisa Su</a>, a key lieutenant who had been serving as chief operating officer since June.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup></p>
<p>On October 16, 2014, AMD announced a new restructuring plan along with its Q3 results. Effective July 1, 2014, AMD reorganized into two business groups: Computing and Graphics, which primarily includes desktop and notebook processors and chipsets, discrete GPUs, and professional graphics; and Enterprise, Embedded and Semi-Custom, which primarily includes server and embedded processors, dense servers, semi-custom SoC products, engineering services, and royalties. As part of this restructuring AMD announced that 7% of its global workforce would be laid off by the end of 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_CPUs_and_APUs">History of CPUs and APUs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Advanced_Micro_Devices&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: History of CPUs and APUs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="IBM_PC_and_the_x86_architecture">IBM PC and the x86 architecture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Advanced_Micro_Devices&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: IBM PC and the x86 architecture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Am286" title="Am286">Am286</a>, <a href="/wiki/Am386" title="Am386">Am386</a>, <a href="/wiki/Am486" title="Am486">Am486</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Am5x86" title="Am5x86">Am5x86</a></div>
<p>In February 1982, AMD signed a contract with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intel_Corporation" title="Intel Corporation">Intel</a>, becoming a licensed second-source manufacturer of <a href="/wiki/Intel_8086" title="Intel 8086">8086</a> and <a href="/wiki/Intel_8088" title="Intel 8088">8088</a> processors. <a href="/wiki/IBM" title="IBM">IBM</a> wanted to use the Intel 8088 in its <a href="/wiki/IBM_Personal_Computer" title="IBM Personal Computer">IBM PC</a>, but IBM's policy at the time was to require at least two sources for its chips. AMD later produced the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Am286" title="Am286">Am286</a> under the same arrangement. In 1984 Intel, in order to shore up its advantage in the marketplace, internally decided to no longer cooperate with AMD in supplying product information, and delayed and eventually refused to convey the technical details of the <a href="/wiki/Intel_80386" title="Intel 80386">Intel 80386</a> to AMD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> In 1987, AMD invoked arbitration over the issue, and Intel reacted by cancelling the 1982 technological-exchange agreement altogether.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> After three years of testimony, AMD eventually won in arbitration in 1992, but Intel disputed this decision. Another long legal dispute followed, ending in 1994 when the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_California" title="Supreme Court of California">Supreme Court of California</a> sided with the arbitrator and AMD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1990, Intel also countersued AMD, reneging on AMD's right to use derivatives of Intel's <a href="/wiki/Microcode" title="Microcode">microcode</a> for its cloned processors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> In the face of uncertainty during the legal dispute, AMD was forced to develop <a href="/wiki/Clean_room_design" title="Clean room design">clean room designed</a> versions of Intel code for its x386 and x486 processors, the former long after Intel had released its own x386 in 1985.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> In March 1991, AMD released the <a href="/wiki/Am386" title="Am386">Am386</a>, its clone of the Intel 386 processor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HittC26_53-4"><a href="#cite_note-HittC26-53">[53]</a></sup> By October of the same year it had sold one million units.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HittC26_53-5"><a href="#cite_note-HittC26-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1993, AMD introduced the first of the <a href="/wiki/Am486" title="Am486">Am486</a> family of processors,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-history_21-3"><a href="#cite_note-history-21">[21]</a></sup> which proved popular with a large number of <a href="/wiki/Original_equipment_manufacturer" title="Original equipment manufacturer">original equipment manufacturers</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Compaq" title="Compaq">Compaq</a>, which signed an exclusive agreement using the Am486.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-directory_13-3"><a href="#cite_note-directory-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> Another Am486-based processor, the <a href="/wiki/Am5x86" title="Am5x86">Am5x86</a>, was released in November 1995 and continued AMD's success as a fast, cost-effective processor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup></p>
<p>Finally, in an agreement effective 1996, AMD received the rights to the microcode in Intel's x386 and x486 processor families, but not the rights to the microcode in the following generations of processors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="K5.2C_K6.2C_Athlon.2C_Duron.2C_and_Sempron"><span id="K5"></span><span id="K6"></span><span id="Athlon"></span><span id="Duron"></span><span id="Sempron"></span>K5, K6, Athlon, Duron, and Sempron</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Advanced_Micro_Devices&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: K5, K6, Athlon, Duron, and Sempron">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/AMD_K5" title="AMD K5">AMD K5</a>, <a href="/wiki/AMD_K6" title="AMD K6">AMD K6</a>, <a href="/wiki/Athlon" title="Athlon">Athlon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Duron" title="Duron">Duron</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sempron" title="Sempron">Sempron</a></div>
<p>AMD's first in-house x86 processor was the <a href="/wiki/AMD_K5" title="AMD K5">K5</a>, which was launched in 1996.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CPU-INFO_K5_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-CPU-INFO_K5-107">[107]</a></sup> The "K" was a reference to <a href="/wiki/Kryptonite" title="Kryptonite">Kryptonite</a>. (In comic books, the only substance which could harm <a href="/wiki/Superman" title="Superman">Superman</a> was Kryptonite. This is a reference to Intel's hegemony over the market, i.e., an anthropomorphization of them as Superman.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Forbes-Chip-Names_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-Forbes-Chip-Names-108">[108]</a></sup>) The numeral "5" refers to the fifth generation of x86 processors; rival Intel had previously introduced its line of fifth-generation x86 processors as <a href="/wiki/Pentium" title="Pentium">Pentium</a> because the U.S. Trademark and Patent Office had ruled that more numbers could not be trademarked.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-newyorker_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-newyorker-109">[109]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1996, AMD purchased <a href="/wiki/NexGen" title="NexGen">NexGen</a>, specifically for the rights to their Nx series of x86-compatible processors. AMD gave the NexGen design team their own building, left them alone, and gave them time and money to rework the Nx686. The result was the <a href="/wiki/AMD_K6" title="AMD K6">K6</a> processor, introduced in 1997. Although the K6 was based on <a href="/wiki/Socket_7" title="Socket 7">Socket 7</a>, variants such as K6-3/450 were faster than Intel's <a href="/wiki/Pentium_II" title="Pentium II">Pentium II</a> (sixth-generation processor).</p>
<p>The K7 was AMD's seventh-generation x86 processor, making its debut on June 23, 1999, under the brand name <a href="/wiki/Athlon" title="Athlon">Athlon</a>. Unlike previous AMD processors, it could not be used on the same motherboards as Intel's, due to licensing issues surrounding Intel's <a href="/wiki/Slot_1" title="Slot 1">Slot 1</a> connector, and instead used a <a href="/wiki/Slot_A" title="Slot A">Slot A</a> connector, referenced to the <a href="/wiki/DEC_Alpha" title="DEC Alpha">Alpha</a> processor bus. The <a href="/wiki/Duron" title="Duron">Duron</a> was a lower-cost and limited version of the Athlon (64KB instead of 256KB L2 cache) in a 462-pin socketed PGA (socket A) or soldered directly onto the motherboard. <a href="/wiki/Sempron" title="Sempron">Sempron</a> was released as a lower-cost Athlon XP, replacing Duron in the <a href="/wiki/Socket_A" title="Socket A">socket A</a> PGA era. It has since been migrated upward to all new sockets, up to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/AM3" title="AM3">AM3</a>.</p>
<p>On October 9, 2001, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Athlon_XP" title="Athlon XP">Athlon XP</a> was released. On February 10, 2003, the Athlon XP with 512KB L2 Cache was released.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_AMD_Athlon_XP_Processor_with_512KB_L2_Cache_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_AMD_Athlon_XP_Processor_with_512KB_L2_Cache-110">[110]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Athlon_64.2C_Opteron_and_Phenom">Athlon 64, Opteron and Phenom</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Advanced_Micro_Devices&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Athlon 64, Opteron and Phenom">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Athlon_64" title="Athlon 64">Athlon 64</a>, <a href="/wiki/Opteron" title="Opteron">Opteron</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phenom_(processor)" title="Phenom (processor)">Phenom (processor)</a></div>
<p>The K8 was a major revision of the K7 architecture, with the most notable features being the addition of a <a href="/wiki/64-bit_computing" title="64-bit computing">64-bit</a> extension to the x86 instruction set (called <a href="/wiki/X86-64" title="X86-64">x86-64</a>, AMD64, or x64), the incorporation of an on-chip memory controller, and the implementation of an extremely high performance point-to-point interconnect called <a href="/wiki/HyperTransport" title="HyperTransport">HyperTransport</a>, as part of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Direct_Connect_Architecture" title="Direct Connect Architecture">Direct Connect Architecture</a>. The technology was initially launched as the <a href="/wiki/Opteron" title="Opteron">Opteron</a> server-oriented processor on April 22, 2003.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> Shortly thereafter it was incorporated into a product for desktop PCs, branded <a href="/wiki/Athlon_64" title="Athlon 64">Athlon 64</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup></p>
<p>On April 21, 2005, AMD released the first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dual_core" title="Dual core">dual core</a> <a href="/wiki/Opteron" title="Opteron">Opteron</a>, an x86-based server CPU.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> A month later, AMD released the <a href="/wiki/Athlon_64_X2" title="Athlon 64 X2">Athlon 64 X2</a>, the first desktop-based <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Multi-core_(computing)" title="Multi-core (computing)">dual core processor</a> family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> In May 2007, AMD abandoned the string "64" in its dual-core desktop product branding, becoming Athlon X2, downplaying the significance of <a href="/wiki/X86-64" title="X86-64">64-bit computing</a> in its processors. Further updates involved improvements to the microarchitecture, and a shift of target market from mainstream desktop systems to value dual-core desktop systems. In 2008, AMD started to release dual-core Sempron processors exclusively in China, branded as the Sempron 2000 series, with lower HyperTransport speed and smaller L2 cache. Thus AMD completed its dual-core product portfolio for each market segment.</p>
<p>After K8 came <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/AMD_K10" title="AMD K10">K10</a>. In September 2007, AMD released the first K10 processors – nine quad-core Third Generation Opteron processors – followed in November by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phenom_(processor)" title="Phenom (processor)">Phenom</a> processor for desktop. K10 processors came in dual-core, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Multi-core" title="Multi-core">triple-core</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Multi-core" title="Multi-core">quad-core</a> versions, with all cores on a single die. AMD released a new platform, codenamed "<a href="/wiki/AMD_Spider" title="AMD Spider">Spider</a>", which utilized the new Phenom processor, as well as an R770 GPU and a 790 GX/FX chipset from the <a href="/wiki/AMD_700_chipset_series" title="AMD 700 chipset series">AMD 700 chipset series</a>. However, AMD built the Spider at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/65nm" title="65nm">65nm</a>, which was uncompetitive with Intel's smaller and more power-efficient <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/45nm" title="45nm">45nm</a>.</p>
<p>In January 2009, AMD released a new processor line dubbed <a href="/wiki/Phenom_II" title="Phenom II">Phenom II</a>, a refresh of the original <a href="/wiki/AMD_Phenom" title="AMD Phenom">Phenom</a> built using the 45 nm process. AMD's new platform, codenamed “<a href="/wiki/AMD_Dragon" title="AMD Dragon">Dragon</a>”, utilised the new Phenom II processor, and an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ATI_(brand)" title="ATI (brand)">ATI</a> R770 GPU from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radeon_R700" title="Radeon R700">R700</a> GPU family, as well as a 790 GX/FX chipset from the <a href="/wiki/AMD_700_chipset_series" title="AMD 700 chipset series">AMD 700 chipset series</a>. The Phenom II came in dual-core, triple-core and quad-core variants, all using the same die, with cores disabled for the triple-core and dual-core versions. The Phenom II resolved issues that the original Phenom had, including a low clock speed, a small L3 cache and a <a href="/wiki/Cool%27n%27Quiet" title="Cool'n'Quiet">Cool'n'Quiet</a> bug that decreased performance. The Phenom II cost less but was not performance-competitive with Intel's mid-to-high-range <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Core_2" title="Core 2">Core 2</a> Quads. The Phenom II also enhanced the Phenom's memory controller, allowing it to use <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/DDR3" title="DDR3">DDR3</a> in a new native socket <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/AM3" title="AM3">AM3</a>, while maintaining backwards compatibility with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/AM2%2B" title="AM2+">AM2+</a>, the socket used for the Phenom, and allowing the use of the <a href="/wiki/DDR2_SDRAM" title="DDR2 SDRAM">DDR2</a> memory that was used with the platform.</p>
<p>In April 2010, AMD released a new Phenom II hexa-core (6-core) processor codenamed "<a href="/wiki/Phenom_II#Thuban" title="Phenom II">Thuban</a>". This was a totally new die based on the hexa-core “Istanbul” <a href="/wiki/Opteron" title="Opteron">Opteron</a> processor. It included AMD's “turbo core” technology, which allows the processor to automatically switch from 6 cores to 3 faster cores when more pure speed is needed. AMD's <a href="/wiki/Enthusiast_computing" title="Enthusiast computing">enthusiast</a> platform, codenamed "Leo", utilized the new Phenom II, a new chipset from the <a href="/wiki/AMD_800_chipset_series" title="AMD 800 chipset series">AMD 800 chipset series</a> and an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ATI_(brand)" title="ATI (brand)">ATI</a> “Cypress” GPU from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Evergreen_(GPU_family)" title="Evergreen (GPU family)">Evergreen GPU</a> series.</p>

<div style="width:52px"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ambox_current_red.svg"><img alt="Ambox current red.svg" data-file-height="290" data-file-width="360" height="34" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Ambox_current_red.svg/42px-Ambox_current_red.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Ambox_current_red.svg/63px-Ambox_current_red.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Ambox_current_red.svg/84px-Ambox_current_red.svg.png 2x" width="42"/></a></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span">This section needs to be <b>updated</b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">Please update this article to reflect recent events or newly available information.</span> <small><i>(July 2014)</i></small></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/List_of_AMD_Opteron_microprocessors#Opteron_6100-series_.22Magny-Cours.22_.2845_nm.29" title="List of AMD Opteron microprocessors">Magny Cours</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_AMD_Opteron_microprocessors#Opteron_4100-series_.22Lisbon.22_.2845_nm.29" title="List of AMD Opteron microprocessors">Lisbon</a> server parts were released in 2010. The Magny Cours part came in 8 to 12 cores and the Lisbon part in 4 and 6 core parts. Magny Cours is focused on performance while the Lisbon part is focused on high performance per watt. Magny Cours is an MCM (<a href="/wiki/Multi-chip_module" title="Multi-chip module">multi-chip module</a>) with two hexa-core “Istanbul” <a href="/wiki/Opteron" title="Opteron">Opteron</a> parts. This will use a new G34 socket for dual and quad socket processors and thus will be marketed as Opteron 61xx series processors. Lisbon uses C32 socket certified for dual socket use or single socket use only and thus will be marketed as Opteron 41xx processors. Both will be built on a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/45_nm" title="45 nm">45 nm</a> SOI process.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fusion_becomes_the_AMD_APU.2C_and_new_microarchitectures">Fusion becomes the AMD APU, and new microarchitectures</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Advanced_Micro_Devices&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Fusion becomes the AMD APU, and new microarchitectures">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/AMD_APU" title="AMD APU">AMD APU</a>, <a href="/wiki/AMD_mobile_platform" title="AMD mobile platform">AMD mobile platform</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bulldozer_(processor)" title="Bulldozer (processor)">The AMD FX and Opteron Bulldozer microarchitecture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bobcat_(microarchitecture)" title="Bobcat (microarchitecture)">The AMD APU Bobcat microarchitecture</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jaguar_(microarchitecture)" title="Jaguar (microarchitecture)">The Jaguar microarchitecture used in multiple processors and APUs</a></div>
<p>Following AMD's 2006 acquisition of Canadian graphics company <a href="/wiki/ATI_Technologies" title="ATI Technologies">ATI Technologies</a>, an initiative codenamed <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/AMD_Fusion" title="AMD Fusion">Fusion</a></i> was announced to integrate a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CPU" title="CPU">CPU</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GPU" title="GPU">GPU</a> together on some of AMD's microprocessors, including a built in <a href="/wiki/PCI_Express" title="PCI Express">PCI Express</a> link to accommodate separate PCI Express peripherals, eliminating the <a href="/wiki/Northbridge_(computing)" title="Northbridge (computing)">northbridge</a> chip from the motherboard. The initiative intended to move some of the processing originally done on the CPU (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Floating-point_unit" title="Floating-point unit">floating-point unit</a> operations) to the GPU, which is better optimized for some calculations. The Fusion was later renamed to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/AMD_APU" title="AMD APU">AMD APU</a> (Accelerated Processing Unit).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-APU_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-APU-116">[116]</a></sup></p>
<p>Llano was AMD's first APU built for <a href="/wiki/Laptop" title="Laptop">laptops</a>. Llano was the second APU released,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hruska_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hruska-117">[117]</a></sup> targeted at the mainstream market.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-APU_116-1"><a href="#cite_note-APU-116">[116]</a></sup> Incorporating a CPU and GPU on the same die, as well as northbridge functions, and using "<a href="/wiki/Socket_FM1" title="Socket FM1">Socket FM1</a>" with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/DDR3" title="DDR3">DDR3</a> memory. The CPU part of the processor was based on the <a href="/wiki/Phenom_II" title="Phenom II">Phenom II</a> "Deneb" processor. AMD suffered an unexpected decrease in revenue based on production problems for the Llano.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup></p>
<p>Bulldozer is AMD's microarchitecture codename for server and desktop <a href="/wiki/AMD_FX" title="AMD FX">AMD FX</a> processors first released on October 12, 2011. This <a href="/wiki/List_of_AMD_CPU_microarchitectures#x86_microarchitectures" title="List of AMD CPU microarchitectures">family 15h microarchitecture</a> is the successor to the <a href="/wiki/AMD_10h" title="AMD 10h">family 10h (K10)</a> microarchitecture design. Bulldozer is designed from scratch, not a development of earlier processors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> The core is specifically aimed at 10-125 <a href="/wiki/Watt" title="Watt">W</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thermal_Design_Power" title="Thermal Design Power">TDP</a> computing products. AMD claims dramatic performance-per-watt efficiency improvements in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High-performance_computing" title="High-performance computing">high-performance computing</a> (HPC) applications with Bulldozer cores. While hopes were very high that Bulldozer would bring AMD to be performance competitive with archrival Intel once more, most benchmarks were disappointing. In some cases the new Bulldozer products were slower than the K10 model they were built to replace.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup></p>
<p>Hondo is AMD's latest processor series used in <a href="/wiki/Tablet_computer" title="Tablet computer">Tablet computers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup></p>
<p>Piledriver is the name of AMD's microarchitecture used in some <a href="/wiki/AMD_FX" title="AMD FX">AMD FX</a> processors released in 2012. This AMD FX series processor lineup is called Vishera, and targets the desktop performance market.</p>
<p>Jaguar is a <a href="/wiki/X86-64" title="X86-64">x86-64</a> microarchitecture codename for a processor core that is used in various APUs from AMD aimed at the low-power/low-cost market. It is also used as the microarchitecture for the custom APUs in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PS4" title="PS4">PS4</a> and <a href="/wiki/Xbox_One" title="Xbox One">Xbox One</a> (which contain CPU, GPU and memory).</p>
<p>Jaguar's predecessor, Bobcat, was revealed during a speech from AMD executive vice-president Henri Richard in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Computex" title="Computex">Computex</a> 2007 and was put into production Q1 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hruska_117-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hruska-117">[117]</a></sup> One of the major supporters was executive vice-president <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mario_A._Rivas" title="Mario A. Rivas">Mario A. Rivas</a> who felt it was difficult to compete in the x86 market with a single core optimized for the 10-100 W range and actively promoted the development of the simpler core with a target range of 1-10 watts. In addition, it was believed that the core could migrate into the hand-held space if the power consumption can be reduced to less than 1 W.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="ARM_architecture-based_chip">ARM architecture-based chip</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Advanced_Micro_Devices&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: ARM architecture-based chip">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Update" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ambox_current_red.svg"><img alt="Ambox current red.svg" data-file-height="290" data-file-width="360" height="34" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Ambox_current_red.svg/42px-Ambox_current_red.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Ambox_current_red.svg/63px-Ambox_current_red.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Ambox_current_red.svg/84px-Ambox_current_red.svg.png 2x" width="42"/></a></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span">This section needs to be <b>updated</b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">Please update this article to reflect recent events or newly available information.</span> <small><i>(April 2016)</i></small></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>AMD intends to release 64-bit <a href="/wiki/ARM_architecture" title="ARM architecture">ARM</a> System on Chips (SoC) that will begin sampling in early 2014 and shipping in the second half of 2015. They will be for use in servers as a low-power alternative to current x86 chips. Their implementation using the ARM architecture is codenamed "Seattle", based on the Cortex A57 core design (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ARMv8-A" title="ARMv8-A">ARMv8-A</a>), and will contain 8 and 16 cores each. They will include the proprietary SeaMicro "Freedom Fabric", as well as support for 128 GB RAM, and 10 gigabit Ethernet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cunningham2013_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-cunningham2013-124">[124]</a></sup> This is to be followed by the custom ARM core <a href="/wiki/AMD_K12" title="AMD K12">K12</a> core, expected in 2016-2017.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Zen_based_CPUs_and_APUs">Zen based CPUs and APUs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Advanced_Micro_Devices&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Zen based CPUs and APUs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Zen_(microarchitecture)" title="Zen (microarchitecture)">Zen (microarchitecture)</a></div>
<p>Zen is a new architecture for x86-64 based CPUs and APUs, built from the ground up by a team led by <a href="/wiki/Jim_Keller_(engineer)" title="Jim Keller (engineer)">Jim Keller</a>, beginning with his arrival in 2012, and <a href="/wiki/Tape-out" title="Tape-out">taping out</a> before his departure in September 2015. Zen will be built on the 14 nm node and have a renewed focus on single-core performance and HSA compatibility.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> Zen will be the first chip encompassing CPUs and APUs from AMD built for a single socket. It will also support DDR4. It is expected to be released in early 2017.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zen_ars_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-zen_ars-127">[127]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_products_and_technologies">Other products and technologies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Advanced_Micro_Devices&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Other products and technologies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
